We first start by introducing the problem, related work and the context of this paper.

Orthogonality constraint has first been considered for fully connected neural networks (Arjovsky et al., 2016). For Convolutional Neural Networks (LeCun and Bengio, 1995; Krizhevsky et al., 2012; Zhang et al., 2015), the introduction of the orthogonality constraint is a way to improve the neural network in several regards. First, despite well-established solutions (He et al., 2016; Ioffe and Szegedy, 2015), the training of very deep convolutional networks remains difficult. This is in particular due to vanishing/exploding gradient problems (Hochreiter, 1991; Bengio et al., 1994). As a result, the expressive capacity of convolutional layers is not fully exploited (Ioffe and Szegedy, 2015). This can lead to lower performances on machine learning tasks. Also, the absence of constraint on the convolutional layer often leads to irregular predictions that are prone to adversarial attacks (Szegedy et al., 2014; Nguyen et al., 2015). Gradient vanishing/exploding avoidance, built-in robustness and better generalization capabilities are the main aims of the introduction of Lipschitz (Szegedy et al., 2014; Qian and Wegman, 2018; Gouk et al., 2021; Tsuzuku et al., 2018; Sedghi et al., 2018) and orthogonality constraints to convolutional layers (Xie et al., 2017; Cisse et al., 2017; Huang et al., 2018; Zhang et al., 2019; Li et al., 2019b; Guo and Ye, 2020; Qi et al., 2020; Wang et al., 2020; Trockman and Kolter, 2021; Jia et al., 2019; Li et al., 2019a; Huang et al., 2020; Jia et al., 2019; Bansal et al., 2018; Xiao et al., 2018).Orthogonal convolutional networks have been applied successfully in diverse applications, such as classification, segmentation, inpainting (Wang et al., 2020; Zhang et al., 2020; Larrazabal et al., 2021), or recently in few-shot learning (Osahor and Nasrabadi, 2022).Orthogonality is also proposed for Generative Adversarial Networks (GAN)(Miyato et al., 2018), or even required for Wasserstein distance estimation, such as in Wasserstein-GAN (Arjovsky and Bottou, 2017; Gulrajani et al., 2017), and Optimal Transport based classifier (Serrurier et al., 2021).

Orthogonal convolutional networks are made of several orthogonal convolutional layers. This means that, when expressing the computation performed by the layer as a matrix, the matrix is orthogonal. The term ’orthogonal’ applies both to square and non-square matrices111The same property is sometimes called ’semi-orthogonal’.. In the latter case, it includes two commonly distinguished but related notions: row-orthogonality and column-orthogonality.This article focuses on the theoretical properties of orthogonal convolutional layers. Furthermore, since deconvolution (also called transposed convolution) layers are defined using convolution layers, the results can also be applied to orthogonal deconvolution layers.We will consider the architecture of a convolutional layer as characterized by (M,C,k,S)𝑀𝐶𝑘𝑆(M,C,k,S), where M𝑀M is the number of output channels, C𝐶C of input channels, convolution kernels are of size k×k𝑘𝑘k\times k and the stride parameter is S𝑆S. Unless we specify otherwise, we consider convolutions with circular boundary conditions222Before computing a convolution the input channels are made periodic outside their genuine support..Thus, applied oninput channels of size S​N×S​N𝑆𝑁𝑆𝑁SN\times SN, the M𝑀M output channels are of size N×N𝑁𝑁N\times N. We denote by 𝐊∈ℝM×C×k×k𝐊superscriptℝ𝑀𝐶𝑘𝑘\mathbf{K}\in\mathbb{R}^{M\times C\times k\times k} the kernel tensor and by 𝒦∈ℝM​N2×C​S2​N2𝒦superscriptℝ𝑀superscript𝑁2𝐶superscript𝑆2superscript𝑁2\mathcal{K}\in\mathbb{R}^{MN^{2}\times CS^{2}N^{2}} the matrix that applies the convolutional layer of architecture (M,C,k,S)𝑀𝐶𝑘𝑆(M,C,k,S) to C𝐶C vectorized channels of size S​N×S​N𝑆𝑁𝑆𝑁SN\times SN.

We will first answer the important questions:•Existence: What is a necessary and sufficient condition on (M,C,k,S)𝑀𝐶𝑘𝑆(M,C,k,S) and N𝑁N such that there exists an orthogonal convolutional layer (i.e. 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} orthogonal) for this architecture? How do the ’valid’ and ’same’ boundary conditions restrict the orthogonality existence?Answers to these questions are respectively in Section 2.1, Theorem 4 and Section 2.2, Proposition 5 and Proposition 6.

Besides, we will rely on recently published papers (Wang et al., 2020; Qi et al., 2020) which characterize orthogonal convolutional layers as the zero level set of a particular function that is called Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} in Wang et al. (2020)333The situation is more complex in (Wang et al., 2020; Qi et al., 2020). One of the contributions of the present paper is to clarify the situation. We describe here the clarified statement. (see Section 1.3.2 for details). Formally, 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} is orthogonal if and only if Lo​r​t​h​(𝐊)=0subscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎ𝐊0L_{orth}(\mathbf{K})=0. They use Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} as a regularization term and obtain impressive performances on several machine learning tasks (see  Wang et al., 2020). The regularization is later successfully applied for medical image segmentation (Zhang et al., 2020), inpainting (Larrazabal et al., 2021) and few-shot learning (Osahor and Nasrabadi, 2022).

In the present paper, we investigate the following theoretical questions:•Stability with regard to minimization errors: Does 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} still have good ‘approximate orthogonality properties’ when Lo​r​t​h​(𝐊)subscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎ𝐊L_{orth}(\mathbf{K}) is small but non zero?Without this guarantee, it could happen that Lo​r​t​h​(𝐊)=10−9subscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎ𝐊superscript109L_{orth}(\mathbf{K})=10^{-9} and ‖𝒦​𝒦T−I​d‖2=109subscriptnorm𝒦superscript𝒦𝑇𝐼𝑑2superscript109\|{\mathcal{K}}{\mathcal{K}}^{T}-Id\|_{2}=10^{9}.This would make the regularization with Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} useless, unless the algorithm reaches Lo​r​t​h​(𝐊)=0subscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎ𝐊0L_{orth}(\mathbf{K})=0.•Scalability and stability with regard to N: Remarking that, for a given kernel tensor 𝐊𝐊\mathbf{K}, Lo​r​t​h​(𝐊)subscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎ𝐊L_{orth}(\mathbf{K}) is independent of N𝑁N but the layer transform matrix 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} depends on N𝑁N: When Lo​r​t​h​(𝐊)subscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎ𝐊L_{orth}(\mathbf{K}) is small, does 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} remain approximately orthogonal and isometric when N𝑁N grows? If so, the regularization with Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} remains efficient even for very large N𝑁N.•Optimization: Does the landscape of Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} lend itself to global optimization?

We give a positive answer to these questions, thus showing theoretical bounds proving that the regularization with Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} is stable (see Theorem 7, Theorem 8 and Section 3.1.3), and can be used in most cases to ensure quasi-orthogonality of the convolutional layers (see Section 3.1.1 and Section 3.1.2).

We describe the related works in Section 1.2 and give the main elements of context in Section 1.3. The theorems constituting the main contributions of the article are in Section 2. Experiments illustrating the theorems, on the landscape of Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth}, as well as experiments showing the benefits of approximate orthogonality on image classification problems are in Section 3. In particular, the latter shows that when orthogonality is used to enforce robustness, the regularization parameter λ𝜆\lambda multiplying Lo​r​t​h​(𝐊)subscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎ𝐊L_{orth}(\mathbf{K}) can be used to tune a tradeoff between accuracy and orthogonality, for the benefit of both accuracy and robustness. The code will be made available in the DEEL.LIP444https://github.com/deel-ai/deel-lip library.

For clarity, we only consider convolutional layers applied to images (2D) in the introduction and the experiments. But we emphasize that the theorems in Section 2 and their proofs are provided for both signals (1D) and images (2D).

Orthogonal matrices form the Stiefel Manifold and were studied in Edelman et al. (1998). In particular, the Stiefel Manifold is compact, smooth and of known dimension. It is made of several connected components. This can be a numerical issue since most algorithms have difficulty changing connected components during optimization. The Stiefel Manifold has many other nice properties that make it suitable for (local) Riemannian optimization (Lezcano-Casado and Martınez-Rubio, 2019; Li et al., 2019a). Orthogonal convolutional layers are a subpart of this Stiefel Manifold. To the best of our knowledge, the understanding of orthogonal convolutional layers is weak. There is no paper focusing on the theoretical properties of orthogonal convolutional layers.

Many articles (Xu and Mannor, 2012; Cisse et al., 2017; Sokolić et al., 2017; Jia et al., 2019; Virmaux and Scaman, 2018; Gouk et al., 2021; Farnia et al., 2018)focus on Lipschitz and orthogonality constraints of the neural network layers from a statistical point of view, in particular in the context of adversarial attacks.

Many recent papers have investigated the numerical problem of optimizing a kernel tensor 𝐊𝐊\mathbf{K} under the constraint that 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} is orthogonal or approximately orthogonal. They also provide modeling arguments and experiments in favor of this constraint. We can distinguish two main strategies: kernel orthogonality (Xie et al., 2017; Cisse et al., 2017; Huang et al., 2018; Zhang et al., 2019; Guo and Ye, 2020; Jia et al., 2019; Li et al., 2019a; Huang et al., 2020; Jia et al., 2019; Bansal et al., 2018; Serrurier et al., 2021) and convolutional layer orthogonality (Li et al., 2019b; Qi et al., 2020; Wang et al., 2020; Trockman and Kolter, 2021; Kiani et al., 2022). The latter has been introduced more recently.

We denote the input of the layer by X∈ℝC×S​N×S​N𝑋superscriptℝ𝐶𝑆𝑁𝑆𝑁X\in\mathbb{R}^{C\times SN\times SN} and its output by Y=𝐜𝐨𝐧𝐯⁡(𝐊,X)∈ℝM×N×N𝑌𝐜𝐨𝐧𝐯𝐊𝑋superscriptℝ𝑀𝑁𝑁Y=\operatorname{{\bf conv}}(\mathbf{K},X)\in\mathbb{R}^{M\times N\times N}.•Kernel Orthogonality: This class of methods views the convolution as a multiplication between a matrix 𝐊¯∈ℝM×C​k2¯𝐊superscriptℝ𝑀𝐶superscript𝑘2\overline{\mathbf{K}}\in\mathbb{R}^{M\times Ck^{2}} formed by reshaping the kernel tensor 𝐊𝐊\mathbf{K}(see, for instance, Cisse et al., 2017; Wang et al., 2020, for more details)and the matrix U​(X)∈ℝC​k2×N2𝑈𝑋superscriptℝ𝐶superscript𝑘2superscript𝑁2U(X)\in\mathbb{R}^{Ck^{2}\times N^{2}} whose columns contain the concatenation of the C𝐶C vectorized patches of X𝑋X needed to compute the M𝑀M output channels at a given spatial position (see  Heide et al., 2015; Yanai et al., 2016).We therefore have, Vect⁡(Y)=Vect⁡(𝐊¯​U​(X))Vect𝑌Vect¯𝐊𝑈𝑋\operatorname{Vect}{(Y)}=\operatorname{Vect}{\left(\overline{\mathbf{K}}U(X)\right)}.The kernel orthogonality strategy enforces the orthogonality of the matrix 𝐊¯¯𝐊\overline{\mathbf{K}}.•Convolutional Layer Orthogonality: This class of methods connects the input and the output of the layer directly by writing Vect⁡(Y)=𝒦​Vect⁡(X)Vect𝑌𝒦Vect𝑋\operatorname{Vect}{(Y)}=\mathcal{K}\operatorname{Vect}{(X)} and enforces the orthogonality of 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K}.The difficulty of this method is that the size of the matrix 𝒦∈ℝM​N2×C​S2​N2𝒦superscriptℝ𝑀superscript𝑁2𝐶superscript𝑆2superscript𝑁2\mathcal{K}\in\mathbb{R}^{MN^{2}\times CS^{2}N^{2}} depends on N𝑁N and can be very large.

Kernel orthogonality provides a numerical strategy whose complexity is independent of N𝑁N.However, kernel orthogonality does not imply that 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} is orthogonal. In a nutshell, the problem is that the composition of an orthogonal embedding555Up to a re-scaling, when considering circular boundary conditions, the mapping U𝑈U is orthogonal. and an orthogonal dimensionality reduction has no reason to be orthogonal. This phenomenon has been observed empirically in Li et al. (2019b) and Jia et al. (2019). The authors of Wang et al. (2020) and Qi et al. (2020) also argue that, when 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} has more columns than rows (row orthogonality), the orthogonality of𝐊¯¯𝐊\overline{\mathbf{K}} is necessary but not sufficient to guarantee 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} orthogonal.Kernel orthogonality and convolutional layer orthogonality are different, the latter better avoids gradient vanishing and feature correlation.

We can distinguish between two numerical ways of enforcing orthogonality during training:•Hard Orthogonality: This method consists in keeping the matrix of interest orthogonal during the whole training process.This can be done either by optimizing on the Stiefel Manifold, or by considering a parameterization of a subset of orthogonal matrices (e.g.,  Li et al., 2019a, b; Trockman and Kolter, 2021; Singla and Feizi, 2021; Huang et al., 2018; Zhang et al., 2019; Kiani et al., 2022).Note that some hard convolutional layer orthogonality methods consider mappings of 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K}, therefore resulting in convolutions with kernels of size larger than k×k𝑘𝑘k\times k.•Soft Orthogonality: Another method to impose orthogonality of matrices during the optimization is to add a regularization of the type ‖W​WT−I‖2superscriptnorm𝑊superscript𝑊𝑇𝐼2\|WW^{T}-I\|^{2} to the loss of the specific task.This regularization penalizes the matrices far from orthogonal (e.g.,  Bansal et al., 2018; Cisse et al., 2017; Qi et al., 2020; Wang et al., 2020; Xie et al., 2017; Guo and Ye, 2020; Jia et al., 2019; Huang et al., 2020).

Note that, unlike Kernel Orthogonality, Convolutional Layer Orthogonality deals directly with 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K}, and thus has a complexity that generally depends on N𝑁N.However, in the context of Soft Convolutional Layer Orthogonality, the authors of Qi et al. (2020); Wang et al. (2020) introduce the regularizer Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} which is independent of N𝑁N (see Section 1.3.2 for details), as a surrogate to ‖𝒦​𝒦T−IdM​N2‖F2subscriptsuperscriptnorm𝒦superscript𝒦𝑇subscriptId𝑀superscript𝑁22𝐹\|\mathcal{K}\mathcal{K}^{T}-\operatorname{Id}_{MN^{2}}\|^{2}_{F} and ‖𝒦T​𝒦−IdC​S2​N2‖F2subscriptsuperscriptnormsuperscript𝒦𝑇𝒦subscriptId𝐶superscript𝑆2superscript𝑁22𝐹\|\mathcal{K}^{T}\mathcal{K}-\operatorname{Id}_{CS^{2}N^{2}}\|^{2}_{F}.In Wang et al. (2020), orthogonal convolutional layers involving a stride are considered for the first time.

The present paper specifies the theory supporting the regularization with Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} and the construction of orthogonal convolutional layers. We give necessary and sufficient conditions on the architecture for the orthogonal convolutional layers to exist (see Theorem 4); we unify the Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} formulation for both Row-Orthogonality and Column-Orthogonality cases (see Definition 1); and prove that the regularization with Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth}: 1/ is stable, i.e. Lo​r​t​h​(𝐊)subscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎ𝐊L_{orth}(\mathbf{K}) is small ⟹𝒦​𝒦T−IdM​N2⟹absent𝒦superscript𝒦𝑇subscriptId𝑀superscript𝑁2\Longrightarrow\leavevmode\nobreak\ \mathcal{K}\mathcal{K}^{T}-\operatorname{Id}_{MN^{2}} is small in various senses (see Theorem 7 and Theorem 8); 2/ leads to an orthogonality error that scales favorably when input signal size N𝑁N grows (see Theorem 8 and Section 3.1.3).We empirically show that, in most cases, the landscape of Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} is such that its minimization can be achieved by Adam (Kingma and Ba, 2015), a standard first-order optimizer (see Section 3.1.1). We also identify and analyse the problematic cases (see Section 3.1.2). We show numerically that approximate orthogonality is preserved when N𝑁N increases (see Section 3.1.3).Finally, we illustrate on Cifar10 and Imagenette data sets how the regularization parameter can be chosen to control the tradeoff between accuracy and orthogonality, for the benefit of both accuracy and robustness (see Section 3.2).

In this section, we describe the context of the article by defining orthogonality, the regularization function Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} and the Frobenius and spectral norms of the orthogonality residuals, which are two measures of approximate orthogonality. We relate the latter to an approximate isometry property whose benefits are listed in Table 1. The main notations defined in this section are reminded in Table 2, in Appendix A.1.

Given a kernel tensor 𝐊𝐊\mathbf{K}, the convolutional layer transform matrix 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} can be written as:𝒦=(ℳ​(𝐊1,1)…ℳ​(𝐊1,C)⋮⋮⋮ℳ​(𝐊M,1)…ℳ​(𝐊M,C))∈ℝM​N2×C​S2​N2,𝒦ℳsubscript𝐊11…ℳsubscript𝐊1𝐶⋮⋮⋮ℳsubscript𝐊𝑀1…ℳsubscript𝐊𝑀𝐶superscriptℝ𝑀superscript𝑁2𝐶superscript𝑆2superscript𝑁2\displaystyle\mathcal{K}=\left(\begin{array}[]{c c c}\mathcal{M}(\mathbf{K}_{1,1})&\ldots&\mathcal{M}(\mathbf{K}_{1,C})\\\vdots&\vdots&\vdots\\\mathcal{M}(\mathbf{K}_{M,1})&\ldots&\mathcal{M}(\mathbf{K}_{M,C})\end{array}\right)\in\mathbb{R}^{MN^{2}\times CS^{2}N^{2}}\;,where ℳ​(𝐊i,j)ℳsubscript𝐊𝑖𝑗\mathcal{M}(\mathbf{K}_{i,j}) is a matrix that computes a strided convolution for the kernel 𝐊i,j=𝐊i,j,:,:subscript𝐊𝑖𝑗subscript𝐊𝑖𝑗::\mathbf{K}_{i,j}=\mathbf{K}_{i,j,:,:}, from the input channel j𝑗j, to the output channel i𝑖i (See Appendix B for details). Notice that we use the ’matlab-colon-notation’,such that 𝐊i,j,:,:=(𝐊i,j,m,n)0≤m,n≤k−1∈ℝk×ksubscript𝐊𝑖𝑗::subscriptsubscript𝐊𝑖𝑗𝑚𝑛formulae-sequence0𝑚𝑛𝑘1superscriptℝ𝑘𝑘\mathbf{K}_{i,j,:,:}=(\mathbf{K}_{i,j,m,n})_{0\leq m,n\leq k-1}\in\mathbb{R}^{k\times k}.

In order to define orthogonal matrices, we need to distinguish two cases:•Row case (RO case). When the size of the input space of 𝒦∈ℝM​N2×C​S2​N2𝒦superscriptℝ𝑀superscript𝑁2𝐶superscript𝑆2superscript𝑁2\mathcal{K}\in\mathbb{R}^{MN^{2}\times CS^{2}N^{2}} is larger than the size of its output space, i.e. M≤C​S2𝑀𝐶superscript𝑆2M\leq CS^{2}, 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} is orthogonal if and only if its rows are normalized and mutually orthogonal. Denoting the identity matrix IdM​N2∈ℝM​N2×M​N2subscriptId𝑀superscript𝑁2superscriptℝ𝑀superscript𝑁2𝑀superscript𝑁2\operatorname{Id}_{MN^{2}}\in\mathbb{R}^{MN^{2}\times MN^{2}}, this is written𝒦​𝒦T=IdM​N2.𝒦superscript𝒦𝑇subscriptId𝑀superscript𝑁2\mathcal{K}\mathcal{K}^{T}=\operatorname{Id}_{MN^{2}}.(1)In this case, the mapping 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} performs a dimensionality reduction.•Column case (CO case). When M≥C​S2𝑀𝐶superscript𝑆2M\geq CS^{2}, 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} is orthogonal if and only if its columns are normalized and mutually orthogonal:𝒦T​𝒦=IdC​S2​N2.superscript𝒦𝑇𝒦subscriptId𝐶superscript𝑆2superscript𝑁2\mathcal{K}^{T}\mathcal{K}=\operatorname{Id}_{CS^{2}N^{2}}.(2)In this case, the mapping 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} is an embedding.Both the RO case and CO case are encountered in practice. When M=C​S2𝑀𝐶superscript𝑆2M=CS^{2}, the matrix 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} is square and if it is orthogonal then both (1) and (2) hold. The matrix 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} is then orthogonal in the usual sense and both 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} and 𝒦Tsuperscript𝒦𝑇\mathcal{K}^{T} are isometric.

In this section, we define a variant of the function Lo​r​t​h:ℝM×C×k×k⟶ℝ:subscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎ⟶superscriptℝ𝑀𝐶𝑘𝑘ℝL_{orth}:\mathbb{R}^{M\times C\times k\times k}\longrightarrow\mathbb{R} defined in Wang et al. (2020); Qi et al. (2020). The purpose of the proposed variant is to unify the properties of Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} in the RO case and CO case.

Reminding that k×k𝑘𝑘k\times k is the size of the convolution kernel, for any h,g∈ℝk×kℎ𝑔superscriptℝ𝑘𝑘h,g\in\mathbb{R}^{k\times k} and any P∈ℕ𝑃ℕP\in\mathbb{N}, we define conv⁡(h,g,padding zero=P,stride=1)∈ℝ(2​P+1)×(2​P+1)convℎ𝑔padding zero𝑃stride1superscriptℝ2𝑃12𝑃1\operatorname{conv}(h,g,\text{padding zero}=P,\text{stride}=1)\in\mathbb{R}^{(2P+1)\times(2P+1)} as the convolution666As is common in machine learning, we do not flip hℎh. between hℎh and the zero-padding of g (see Figure 1).Formally, for all i,j∈⟦0,2​P⟧𝑖𝑗02𝑃i,j\in\llbracket 0,2P\rrbracket,[conv⁡(h,g,padding zero=P,stride=1)]i,j=∑i′=0k−1∑j′=0k−1hi′,j′​g¯i+i′,j+j′,subscriptdelimited-[]convℎ𝑔padding zero𝑃stride1𝑖𝑗superscriptsubscriptsuperscript𝑖′0𝑘1superscriptsubscriptsuperscript𝑗′0𝑘1subscriptℎsuperscript𝑖′superscript𝑗′subscript¯𝑔𝑖superscript𝑖′𝑗superscript𝑗′[\operatorname{conv}(h,g,\text{padding zero}=P,\text{stride}=1)]_{i,j}=\sum_{i^{\prime}=0}^{k-1}\sum_{j^{\prime}=0}^{k-1}h_{i^{\prime},j^{\prime}}\bar{g}_{i+i^{\prime},j+j^{\prime}},where g¯∈ℝ(k+2​P)×(k+2​P)¯𝑔superscriptℝ𝑘2𝑃𝑘2𝑃\bar{g}\in\mathbb{R}^{(k+2P)\times(k+2P)} is defined, for all (i,j)∈⟦0,k+2​P−1⟧2𝑖𝑗superscript0𝑘2𝑃12(i,j)\in\llbracket 0,k+2P-1\rrbracket^{2}, byg¯i,j={gi−P,j−Pif ​(i,j)∈⟦P,P+k−1⟧2,0otherwise.subscript¯𝑔𝑖𝑗casessubscript𝑔𝑖𝑃𝑗𝑃if 𝑖𝑗superscript𝑃𝑃𝑘120otherwise\bar{g}_{i,j}=\left\{\begin{array}[]{ll}g_{i-P,j-P}&\mbox{if }(i,j)\in\llbracket P,P+k-1\rrbracket^{2},\\0&\mbox{otherwise}.\end{array}\right.

Of course, when doing so, even if the optimization is efficient, we expect Lo​r​t​h​(𝐊l)subscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎsubscript𝐊𝑙L_{orth}(\mathbf{K}_{l}) to be different from 00 butless than ε𝜀\varepsilon,for a small ε𝜀\varepsilon.We investigate, in this article, whether, in this case, the transformation matrix 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K}, still satisfies useful orthogonality properties.To quantify how much 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} deviates from being orthogonal, we define the approximate orthogonality criteria and approximate isometry property in the next section.These notions allow to statethe stability and scalability theorems (Sections 2.3 and 2.4) and guarantee that the singular values remain close to 111 when Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} is small, even when N𝑁N is large.This proves that the benefits related to the orthogonality of the layers, which are presented in Table 1, still hold.

Perfect orthogonality is an idealization that never happens, due to floating-point arithmetic, and numerical and optimization errors. In order to measure how 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} deviates from being orthogonal, we define the orthogonality residual by .(8)However, the link is weak, when min⁡(a,b)𝑎𝑏\min(a,b) is large.

The regularization with (errNF⁡(𝐊))2superscriptsubscriptsuperscripterr𝐹𝑁𝐊2(\operatorname{err}^{F}_{N}(\mathbf{K}))^{2} is a natural way to enforce soft-orthogonality of 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K}. However, as mentioned in the introduction, it is not practical because the sizes of 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} are too large. We will see in Theorem 7 that (errNF⁡(𝐊))2superscriptsubscriptsuperscripterr𝐹𝑁𝐊2(\operatorname{err}^{F}_{N}(\mathbf{K}))^{2} and Lo​r​t​h​(𝐊)subscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎ𝐊L_{orth}(\mathbf{K}) differ by a multiplicative constant and it will make a clear connection between the regularization with Lo​r​t​h​(𝐊)subscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎ𝐊L_{orth}(\mathbf{K}) and the regularization with (errNF⁡(𝐊))2superscriptsubscriptsuperscripterr𝐹𝑁𝐊2(\operatorname{err}^{F}_{N}(\mathbf{K}))^{2}. However, errNF⁡(𝐊)subscriptsuperscripterr𝐹𝑁𝐊\operatorname{err}^{F}_{N}(\mathbf{K}) is difficult to interpret, this is why we consider errNs⁡(𝐊)subscriptsuperscripterr𝑠𝑁𝐊\operatorname{err}^{s}_{N}(\mathbf{K}) which relates to the approximate isometry property of 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} as we explain below. The latter has direct consequences on the properties of the layer(see Table 1).

Indeed, in the applications, one key property of orthogonal operators is their connection to isometries. It is the property that prevents the gradient from exploding and vanishing (Cisse et al., 2017; Xiao et al., 2018; Li et al., 2019a; Huang et al., 2020). This property also enables to keep the examples well separated, which has an effect similar to the batch normalization (Qi et al., 2020; Chai et al., 2020), and to have a 111-Lipschitz forward pass and therefore improves robustness (Wang et al., 2020; Cisse et al., 2017; Li et al., 2019b; Trockman and Kolter, 2021; Jia et al., 2019).

We denote the Euclidean norm of a vector by ∥.∥\|.\|.To clarify the connection between orthogonality and isometry, we define the ‘ε𝜀\varepsilon-Approximate Isometry Property’ (ε𝜀\varepsilon-AIP).

The following proposition makes the link between errNs⁡(𝐊)subscriptsuperscripterr𝑠𝑁𝐊\operatorname{err}^{s}_{N}(\mathbf{K}) and AIP. It shows that minimizing errNs⁡(𝐊)subscriptsuperscripterr𝑠𝑁𝐊\operatorname{err}^{s}_{N}(\mathbf{K}) enhances the AIP property.

This statement actually holds for any matrix (not only layer transform matrix) and is already stated in Bansal et al. (2018); Guo and Ye (2020). For completeness, we provide proof, in Appendix G.

In Proposition 3 and in Theorem 4 (see the next section), the condition S​N≥k𝑆𝑁𝑘SN\geq k only states that the inputwidth and height are larger than the size of the kernels. This is always the case in practice.

We summarize in Table 1 the properties of ε𝜀\varepsilon-AIP layers when ε𝜀\varepsilon is small, in the different possible scenarios.We remind that a kernel tensor 𝐊𝐊\mathbf{K} can define a convolutional layer or a deconvolution layer. Deconvolution layers are, for instance, used to define layers of the decoder of an auto-encoder or variational auto-encoder (Kingma and Welling, 2014). In the convolutional case, 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} is applied during the forward pass and 𝒦Tsuperscript𝒦𝑇\mathcal{K}^{T} is applied during the backward pass. In a deconvolution layer, 𝒦Tsuperscript𝒦𝑇\mathcal{K}^{T} is applied during the forward pass and 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} during the backward pass. Depending on whether we have M<C​S2𝑀𝐶superscript𝑆2M<CS^{2}, M>C​S2𝑀𝐶superscript𝑆2M>CS^{2} or M=C​S2𝑀𝐶superscript𝑆2M=CS^{2}, when 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} is ε𝜀\varepsilon-AIP with ε<<1much-less-than𝜀1\varepsilon<<1, either 𝒦Tsuperscript𝒦𝑇\mathcal{K}^{T}, 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} or both preserve distances (see Table 1).

To complement Table 1, notice that in the RO case, if errNF⁡(𝐊)≤εsubscriptsuperscripterr𝐹𝑁𝐊𝜀\operatorname{err}^{F}_{N}(\mathbf{K})\leq\varepsilon, then for any i𝑖i, j𝑗j with i≠j𝑖𝑗i\neq j, we have |𝒦i,:​𝒦j,:T|≤εsubscript𝒦𝑖:subscriptsuperscript𝒦𝑇𝑗:𝜀|\mathcal{K}_{i,:}\mathcal{K}^{T}_{j,:}|\leq\varepsilon, where 𝒦i,:subscript𝒦𝑖:\mathcal{K}_{i,:} is the ithsuperscript𝑖thi^{\mbox{th}} line of 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K}. In other words, when ε𝜀\varepsilon is small, the features computed by 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} are mostly uncorrelated (Wang et al., 2020).

This section contains the theoretical contributions of the article. In all the theorems in this section, the considered convolutional layers are either applied to a signal, when d=1𝑑1d=1, or an image, when d=2𝑑2d=2.

We remind that the architecture of the layer is characterized by (M,C,k,S)𝑀𝐶𝑘𝑆(M,C,k,S) where: M𝑀M is the number of output channels; C𝐶C is the number of input channels; k≥1𝑘1k\geq 1 is an odd positive integer and the convolution kernels are of size k𝑘k, when d=1𝑑1d=1, and k×k𝑘𝑘k\times k, when d=2𝑑2d=2; the stride parameter is S𝑆S.

We want to highlight that the theorems of Sections 2.1, 2.3 and 2.4 are for convolution operators defined with circular boundary conditions (see Appendix B for details).We point out in Section 2.2 restrictions for the ‘valid’ and ‘same’ zero-padding boundary conditions (see Appendix D.1 and Appendix D.2 for details).

With circular boundary conditions, all input channels are of size S​N𝑆𝑁SN, when d=1𝑑1d=1, S​N×S​N𝑆𝑁𝑆𝑁SN\times SN, when d=2𝑑2d=2. The output channels are of size N𝑁N and N×N𝑁𝑁N\times N, respectively when d=1𝑑1d=1 and 222.When d=1𝑑1d=1, the definitions of Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth}, errNFsubscriptsuperscripterr𝐹𝑁\operatorname{err}^{F}_{N} and errNssubscriptsuperscripterr𝑠𝑁\operatorname{err}^{s}_{N} are in Appendix A.2.

In Section 2.1, we state a theorem that provides the necessary and sufficient conditions on the architecture for an orthogonal convolutional layer to exist. In Section 2.2, we describe restrictions for the ’valid’ and ’same’ boundary conditions.In Section 2.3, we state a theorem that provides a relation between the Frobenius norm of the orthogonality residual and the regularization penalty Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth}.Finally, in Section 2.4, we state a theorem that provides an upper bound of the spectral norm of the orthogonality residual using the regularization penalty Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth}.

The next theorem gives a necessary and sufficient condition on the architecture(M,C,k,S)𝑀𝐶𝑘𝑆(M,C,k,S) and N𝑁N for an orthogonal convolutional layer transformto exist. To simplify notations, we denote, for d=1𝑑1d=1 or 222, the space of all the kernel tensors by𝕂d={ℝM×C×kwhen ​d=1,ℝM×C×k×kwhen ​d=2.subscript𝕂𝑑casessuperscriptℝ𝑀𝐶𝑘when 𝑑1superscriptℝ𝑀𝐶𝑘𝑘when 𝑑2\mathbb{K}_{d}=\left\{\begin{array}[]{ll}\mathbb{R}^{M\times C\times k}&\mbox{when }d=1,\\\mathbb{R}^{M\times C\times k\times k}&\mbox{when }d=2.\end{array}\right.

We also denote, for d=1𝑑1d=1 or 222,𝕂d⟂={𝐊∈𝕂d|𝒦​ is orthogonal}.subscriptsuperscript𝕂perpendicular-to𝑑conditional-set𝐊subscript𝕂𝑑𝒦 is orthogonal\mathbb{K}^{\perp}_{d}=\{\mathbf{K}\in\mathbb{K}_{d}|\leavevmode\nobreak\ \mathcal{K}\mbox{ is orthogonal}\}.

Theorem 4 is proved in Appendix C.Again, the conditions coincide when M=C​Sd𝑀𝐶superscript𝑆𝑑M=CS^{d}.

When S≤k𝑆𝑘S\leq k, which is by far the most common situation, there exist orthogonal convolutional layers in both the CO case and the RO case. Indeed, in the RO case, when S≤k𝑆𝑘S\leq k, we have M≤C​Sd≤C​kd𝑀𝐶superscript𝑆𝑑𝐶superscript𝑘𝑑M\leq CS^{d}\leq Ck^{d}.

However, skip-connection layers (also called shortcut connection) with stride in Resnet (He et al., 2016) for instance, usually have an architecture (M,C,k,S)𝑀𝐶𝑘𝑆(M,C,k,S) = (2​C,C,1,2)2𝐶𝐶12(2C,C,1,2), where C𝐶C is the number of input channels.The kernels are of size 1×1111\times 1. In that case, M≤C​Sd𝑀𝐶superscript𝑆𝑑M\leq CS^{d} and M>C​kd𝑀𝐶superscript𝑘𝑑M>Ck^{d}. Theorem 4 says that there is no orthogonal convolutional layer for this type of layer.

To conclude, the main consequence of Theorem 4 is that, with circular boundary conditions and for most of the architecture used in practice (with an exception for the skip-connections with stride), there exist orthogonal convolutional layers.

In Sections 2.1, 2.3 and 2.4, we consider convolutions defined with circular boundary conditions.This choice is neither for technical reasons nor to enable the use of the Fourier basis.We illustrate in the next two propositions that, for convolutions defined with the ’valid’ condition, or the ’same’ condition with zero-padding, hard-orthogonality is in many situations too restrictive. We consider in this section an unstrided convolution.

Before stating the next proposition, we remind that with the ’valid’ boundary conditions, only the entries of the output such that the support of the translated kernel is entirely included in the input’s domain are computed. The size of eachoutput channel is smaller than the size of the input channels. The formal definition of the ’valid’ boundary conditions is at the beginning of Appendix D.1.

This proposition holds in the 1D and 2D cases.We give its proof only in the 1D case in Appendix D.1.

Before stating the next proposition, we remind that to compute a convolution with the zero-padding ’same’ boundary conditions, we first extend each input channel with zeros and then compute the convolutions such that each output channel has the same support as the input channels, before padding/extension. A formal definition of the zero-padding ’same’ boundary condition is at the beginning of Appendix D.2.

Let (ei,j)i=0..k−1,j=0..k−1{(e_{i,j})}_{i=0..k-1,j=0..k-1} be the canonical basis of ℝk×ksuperscriptℝ𝑘𝑘\mathbb{R}^{k\times k}. For the zero-padding ’same’, we have the following proposition.

This proposition holds in the 1D and 2D cases.We give its proof only in the 1D case in Appendix D.2.

To recapitulate, the results state that with padding ’valid’, no orthogonal convolution can be built in the CO case and that for zero-padding ’same’, the orthogonal convolution layers are trivial transformations.

Note that, the propositions do not exclude the existence of sufficiently expressive sets of ’approximately orthogonal’ convolutional layers with these boundary conditions. A strategy based on soft-orthogonality may still enjoy most of the benefits of orthogonality for these boundary conditions.

We recall that the motivation behind this is the following :The authors of Wang et al. (2020); Qi et al. (2020) argue that Lo​r​t​h​(𝐊)=0subscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎ𝐊0L_{orth}(\mathbf{K})=0 is equivalent to 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} being orthogonal. However, they do not provide stability guarantees. Without this guarantee, it could happen that Lo​r​t​h​(𝐊)=10−9subscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎ𝐊superscript109L_{orth}(\mathbf{K})=10^{-9} and ‖𝒦​𝒦T−I​d‖F=109subscriptnorm𝒦superscript𝒦𝑇𝐼𝑑𝐹superscript109\|{\mathcal{K}}{\mathcal{K}}^{T}-Id\|_{F}=10^{9}. This would make the regularization with Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} useless, unless the algorithm reaches Lo​r​t​h​(𝐊)=0subscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎ𝐊0L_{orth}(\mathbf{K})=0.

The following theorem proves that thiscannot occur.Therefore, if Lo​r​t​h​(𝐊)subscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎ𝐊L_{orth}(\mathbf{K}) is small, errNF⁡(𝐊)subscriptsuperscripterr𝐹𝑁𝐊\operatorname{err}^{F}_{N}(\mathbf{K}) is small at least for moderate signal sizes. Also, a corollary is that adding Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} as a penalty regularization is equivalent to adding the Frobenius norm of the orthogonality residual.

Theorem 7 is proved, in Appendix E.We remind that Lo​r​t​h​(𝐊)subscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎ𝐊L_{orth}(\mathbf{K}) is independent of N𝑁N.The theorem formalizes for circular boundary conditions and for both the CO case and the RO case, the reasoning leading to the regularization with Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} in Wang et al. (2020).

Using Theorem 7, we find that (5) becomesLt​a​s​k+∑lλNld​(errNlF⁡(𝐊l))2.subscript𝐿𝑡𝑎𝑠𝑘subscript𝑙𝜆superscriptsubscript𝑁𝑙𝑑superscriptsubscriptsuperscripterr𝐹subscript𝑁𝑙subscript𝐊𝑙2L_{task}+\sum_{l}\frac{\lambda}{N_{l}^{d}}(\operatorname{err}^{F}_{N_{l}}(\mathbf{K}_{l}))^{2}.Once the parameter λ𝜆\lambda is made dependent on the input size of layer l𝑙l, the regularization term λ​Lo​r​t​h𝜆subscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎ\lambda L_{orth} is equal to the Frobenius norm of the orthogonality residual. This justifies the use of Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} as a regularizer.

We can also see from Theorem 7 that, for both the RO case and the CO case, when Lo​r​t​h​(𝐊)=0subscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎ𝐊0L_{orth}(\mathbf{K})=0, 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} is orthogonal, independently of N𝑁N.This recovers the result stated in Qi et al. (2020) for S=1𝑆1S=1, and the result stated in Wang et al. (2020) in the RO case for any S𝑆S .

Considering another signal size N′superscript𝑁′N^{\prime} and applying Theorem 7 with the sizes N𝑁N and N′superscript𝑁′N^{\prime}, we find(errN′F⁡(𝐊))2=(N′)dNd​(errNF⁡(𝐊))2.superscriptsubscriptsuperscripterr𝐹superscript𝑁′𝐊2superscriptsuperscript𝑁′𝑑superscript𝑁𝑑superscriptsubscriptsuperscripterr𝐹𝑁𝐊2(\operatorname{err}^{F}_{N^{\prime}}(\mathbf{K}))^{2}=\frac{(N^{\prime})^{d}}{N^{d}}(\operatorname{err}^{F}_{N}(\mathbf{K}))^{2}.To the best of our knowledge, this equality is new. This could be of importance in situations when N𝑁N varies. For instance when the neural network is learned on a data set containing signals/images of a given size, but the inference is done for signals/images of varying size (Ren et al., 2015; Shelhamer et al., 2017; Ji et al., 2019).

Finally, using (8) and Proposition 3, 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} is ϵitalic-ϵ\epsilon-AIP with ϵitalic-ϵ\epsilon scaling like the square root of the signal/image size. This might not be satisfactory. We exhibit in the next section a tighter bound on ϵitalic-ϵ\epsilon, independent of the input size N𝑁N.

We prove in Theorem 8 that errNs(𝐊)2\operatorname{err}^{s}_{N}(\mathbf{K})^{2} is sandwiched between two quantities proportional to Lo​r​t​h​(𝐊)subscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎ𝐊L_{orth}(\mathbf{K}). The multiplicative factors do not depend on N𝑁N. Hence, when Lo​r​t​h​(𝐊)subscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎ𝐊L_{orth}(\mathbf{K}) is small, errNs(𝐊)2\operatorname{err}^{s}_{N}(\mathbf{K})^{2} is also small for all N𝑁N. As a consequence, as long as Lo​r​t​h​(𝐊)≪1much-less-thansubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎ𝐊1L_{orth}(\mathbf{K})\ll 1 even if the algorithm does not reach Lo​r​t​h​(𝐊)=0subscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎ𝐊0L_{orth}(\mathbf{K})=0, regularizing with Lo​r​t​h​(𝐊)subscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎ𝐊L_{orth}(\mathbf{K}) permits to construct nearly orthogonal and isometric convolutional layers independently of N𝑁N.

Moreover, combined with Proposition 3 this ensures that, if Lo​r​t​h​(𝐊)subscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎ𝐊L_{orth}(\mathbf{K}) is small, 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} is ε𝜀\varepsilon-AIP with ε𝜀\varepsilon small. Using Table 1, we see that this property leads to more robustness and avoids gradient vanishing/exploding. This is in line with the empirical results observed in Wang et al. (2020); Qi et al. (2020).

Theorem 8 is proved, in Appendix F.When M=C​Sd𝑀𝐶superscript𝑆𝑑M=CS^{d}, the two inequalities hold and it is possible to take the minimum of the two α𝛼\alpha values.

As we can see from Theorem 8, unlike with the Frobenius norm, the spectral norm of the orthogonality residual is upper-bounded by a quantity that does not depend on N𝑁N. The lower-bound of Theorem 8 guarantees that the upper-bound is tight up to a multiplicative constant. Before illustrating the benefits of approximate orthogonality to robustly classify images in Section 3.2, we conduct several synthetic experiments in Section 3.1. The synthetic experiments empirically evaluate the landscape of Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} in Section 3.1.1, 3.1.2 and illustrate the theorems of Section 2, in Section 3.1.3.

Both in Section 3.1 and Section 3.2, to evaluate how close 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} is to being orthogonal, we compute some of its singular values σ𝜎\sigma for different input sizes S​N×S​N𝑆𝑁𝑆𝑁SN\times SN. When S=1𝑆1S=1, we compute all the singular values of 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} with the Algorithm 1, Appendix I, from Sedghi et al. (2018). For convolutions with stride, S>1𝑆1S>1, there is no known practical algorithm to compute all the singular values and we simply apply the well-known power iteration algorithm associated with a spectral shift, to retrieve the smallest and largest singular values (σm​i​n,σm​a​x)subscript𝜎𝑚𝑖𝑛subscript𝜎𝑚𝑎𝑥(\sigma_{min},\sigma_{max}) of 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} (see Algorithm 2 in Appendix I).We remind that 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} orthogonal is equivalent to σm​i​n=σm​a​x=1subscript𝜎𝑚𝑖𝑛subscript𝜎𝑚𝑎𝑥1\sigma_{min}=\sigma_{max}=1.

The right plot of Figure 2 shows that all configurations where M≠C​S2𝑀𝐶superscript𝑆2M\neq CS^{2} are trained very accurately to near-perfect orthogonal convolutions. These architectures, corresponding to a square 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K}, can mostly be found when M=C𝑀𝐶M=C and S=1𝑆1S=1, for instance in VGG (Simonyan and Zisserman, 2015) and Resnet (He et al., 2016).We have conducted experiments that we do not report here in detail, and it seems that this is specific to the convolutional case. Fully-connected layers optimized to be orthogonal do not suffer from this phenomenon.

Since we know that 𝕂2⟂≠∅subscriptsuperscript𝕂perpendicular-to2\mathbb{K}^{\perp}_{2}\neq\emptyset, the explanation for the failure cases (when σm​a​xsubscript𝜎𝑚𝑎𝑥\sigma_{max} or σm​i​nsubscript𝜎𝑚𝑖𝑛\sigma_{min} significantly differ from 111) isthat the optimization was not successful.We tried many learning rate schemes and iteration numbers but obtained similar results101010See the description of the experiments at the beginning of Section 3.1 and the related footnotes..

In the experiment on the left, the optimization is successful and the singular values are very accurately concentrated around 111. On the right, we see that only a few of the singular values significantly differ from 111.

Figure 3 shows that even if σm​i​nsubscript𝜎𝑚𝑖𝑛\sigma_{min} and σm​a​xsubscript𝜎𝑚𝑎𝑥\sigma_{max} are not close to 1, as shown in Figure 2, most of the singular values are close to 1. This probably explains why the landscape problem does not alter the performance on real data sets in Wang et al. (2020) and Qi et al. (2020). Notice that Wang et al. (2020) display a curve similar to Figure 3 when used for a real data set.

In this experiment, we evaluate how the singular values σm​i​nsubscript𝜎𝑚𝑖𝑛\sigma_{min} and σm​a​xsubscript𝜎𝑚𝑎𝑥\sigma_{max} of 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} vary when the parameter N𝑁N defining the size S​N×S​N𝑆𝑁𝑆𝑁SN\times SN of the input channels varies, for 𝐊𝐊\mathbf{K} fixed. This is important for applications (Shelhamer et al., 2017; Ji et al., 2019; Ren et al., 2015) using fully convolutional networks, or for transfer learning using pre-learnt convolutional feature extractor.

To do so, we randomly select 505050 experiments for which the optimization was successful (Lo​r​t​h​(𝐊)≤0.001subscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎ𝐊0.001L_{orth}(\mathbf{K})\leq 0.001) and 505050 experiments for which it was unsuccessful (Lo​r​t​h​(𝐊)≥0.02subscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎ𝐊0.02L_{orth}(\mathbf{K})\geq 0.02). They are respectively used to construct the figures on the left and the right side of Figure 4. For a given 𝐊𝐊\mathbf{K}, we display the singular values σm​i​nsubscript𝜎𝑚𝑖𝑛\sigma_{min} and σm​a​xsubscript𝜎𝑚𝑎𝑥\sigma_{max} of 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} for N∈{5,12,15,32,64,128,256,512,1024}𝑁5121532641282565121024N\in\{5,12,15,32,64,128,256,512,1024\}, as orange and blue dots. The dots corresponding to the same 𝐊𝐊\mathbf{K} are linked by a line.

We see, on the left of Figure 4, that for successful experiments (Lo​r​t​h​(𝐊)≪1much-less-thansubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎ𝐊1L_{orth}(\mathbf{K})\ll 1), the singular values are very stable when N𝑁N varies. This corresponds to the behavior described in Theorem 8 and Proposition 3. We also point out, on the right of Figure 4, that for unsuccessful optimization (Lo​r​t​h≪̸1not-much-less-thansubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎ1L_{orth}\not\ll 1), σm​i​nsubscript𝜎𝑚𝑖𝑛\sigma_{min} (resp. σm​a​xsubscript𝜎𝑚𝑎𝑥\sigma_{max}) values decrease (resp. increase) rapidly when N increases.

In this section we compare, on Cifar10 and Imagenette data sets, the performance, robustness, spectral properties and processing time of three networks: standard convolutional neural networks with unconstrained convolutions called Conv2D, the same network architectures with convolutions regularized with Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} and the same network architectures with convolutions constrained with a method that we call Cayley, a hard convolutional layer orthogonality method111111Our experiments complement the comparison of Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} with kernel orthogonality methods in Wang et al. (2020). based on the Cayley transform (Trockman and Kolter, 2021). The latter builds convolutions parameterized by k×k𝑘𝑘k\times k parameters but, because a mapping is applied to obtain orthogonality, the convolution kernels are of size N×N𝑁𝑁N\times N. In comparison, Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} regularization provides convolutions kernels of size k×k𝑘𝑘k\times k, as is standard. The methods are therefore not expected to provide the same results which makes the comparison a bit complicated. This comparison is also somewhat unfair since the regularization with Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} enjoys a parameter λ𝜆\lambda. We show results for a wide range of λ𝜆\lambda but assume, when interpreting the results, that an optimal λ𝜆\lambda is chosen, for instance using cross-validation.

The design of the experiments aims at simultaneously obtaining good accuracy and robustness. Therefore, for the purpose of robustness, we only use isometric activations and nearly orthogonal convolutional layers. We cannot expect, with this robustness constraint, to obtain clean accuracies as good as those reported in Wang et al. (2020).

On Cifar10, we use a VGG-like architecture (Simonyan and Zisserman, 2015) with nine convolutional layers and a single dense output layer with ten 111-Lipschitz neurons. In all experiments, for a fair comparison, we use invertible downsampling emulation as in Trockman and Kolter (2021). In order to avoid problematic configurations described in Section 3.1.2, we alternate channels numbers C,C+2,C𝐶𝐶2𝐶C,C+2,C within each VGG block (see Table  3).

The network is trained during 400 epochs with a batch size of 128, using cross-entropy loss with temperature, Adam optimizer (Kingma and Ba, 2015) with a decreasing learning rate, and standard data augmentation. A full description of hyperparameters is given in Appendix H.1. The optimized network achieves 91%percent9191\% accuracy on the Cifar10 test set, for the C​o​n​v​2​D𝐶𝑜𝑛𝑣2𝐷Conv2D classical network.

As already mentioned, three configurations are compared: C​o​n​v​2​D𝐶𝑜𝑛𝑣2𝐷Conv2D: classical convolutions (i.e. no regularization), C​a​y​l​e​y𝐶𝑎𝑦𝑙𝑒𝑦Cayley: convolutions constrained by Cayley method (Trockman and Kolter, 2021), and Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth}: the regularization with Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth}. For the Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} regularization, we investigate the properties of the solution obtained when minimizing (5) for λ∈{10,1,10−1,10−2,10−3,10−4,10−5}𝜆101superscript101superscript102superscript103superscript104superscript105\lambda\in\{10,1,10^{-1},10^{-2},10^{-3},10^{-4},10^{-5}\}.

After training, σm​a​xsubscript𝜎𝑚𝑎𝑥\sigma_{max} and σm​i​nsubscript𝜎𝑚𝑖𝑛\sigma_{min} values are computed for each convolutional layer using the method described in Appendix I. Each configuration is learnt 10 times to provide mean and standard deviation for the following metrics:•Acc. clean: Classical accuracy on a clean test set•Σm​a​x=maxl⁡(σm​a​x​(𝒦l))subscriptΣ𝑚𝑎𝑥subscript𝑙subscript𝜎𝑚𝑎𝑥subscript𝒦𝑙\Sigma_{max}=\max_{l}(\sigma_{max}(\mathcal{K}_{l})): the largest singular value among all the convolutional layers’s singular values.•Σm​i​n=minl⁡(σm​i​n​(𝒦l))subscriptΣ𝑚𝑖𝑛subscript𝑙subscript𝜎𝑚𝑖𝑛subscript𝒦𝑙\Sigma_{min}=\min_{l}(\sigma_{min}(\mathcal{K}_{l})): the smallest singular value among all the convolutional layers’s singular values.•El​i​psubscript𝐸𝑙𝑖𝑝E_{lip}: Empirical local Lipschitz constants of the network computed using the PGD-like method proposed by Yang et al. (2020).•Er​o​bsubscript𝐸𝑟𝑜𝑏E_{rob}: The empirical robustness accuracy, i.e. the proportion of test samples on which a vanilla Projected Gradient Descent (PGD) attack (Madry et al., 2018) failed (for a robustness radius ϵ=36/255italic-ϵ36255\epsilon=36/255). PGD attack is applied with 10 iterations and a factor α=ϵ/4.0𝛼italic-ϵ4.0\alpha=\epsilon/4.0 .•Te​p​o​c​hsubscript𝑇𝑒𝑝𝑜𝑐ℎT_{epoch}: the average epoch processing time.

Figure 5 shows that the regularization parameter λ𝜆\lambda, in (5), provides a way to tune a tradeoff between robustness (Er​o​bsubscript𝐸𝑟𝑜𝑏E_{rob}) and clean accuracy (A​c​c.c​l​e​a​nformulae-sequence𝐴𝑐𝑐𝑐𝑙𝑒𝑎𝑛Acc.clean), by controlling the singular values of the layers (Σm​a​xsubscriptΣ𝑚𝑎𝑥\Sigma_{max} and Σm​i​nsubscriptΣ𝑚𝑖𝑛\Sigma_{min}). On the contrary C​a​y​l​e​y𝐶𝑎𝑦𝑙𝑒𝑦Cayley or C​o​n​v​2​D𝐶𝑜𝑛𝑣2𝐷Conv2D each provide a single tradeoff (shown with constant value in figures).The configurations λ=10−1​ and ​10−2𝜆superscript101 and superscript102\lambda=10^{-1}\text{ and }10^{-2} achieve better clean accuracy and similar empirical robustness performances as the C​a​y​l​e​y𝐶𝑎𝑦𝑙𝑒𝑦Cayley method. Furthermore, their empirical Lipschitz constants are very close to one. Finally, error bands for C​a​y​l​e​y𝐶𝑎𝑦𝑙𝑒𝑦Cayley and Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} methods are very narrow.

Processing time Te​p​o​c​hsubscript𝑇𝑒𝑝𝑜𝑐ℎT_{epoch} for regularizing with Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} is only 5%percent55\% slower than the reference network C​o​n​v​2​D𝐶𝑜𝑛𝑣2𝐷Conv2D, but 2.22.22.2 times faster than the one for the C​a​y​l​e​y𝐶𝑎𝑦𝑙𝑒𝑦Cayley method. It is not reported here in detail but the convergence speeds, in number of epochs, are similar.Moreover, Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} provides classical convolution at inference. On the contrary, the C​a​y​l​e​y𝐶𝑎𝑦𝑙𝑒𝑦Cayley method provides orthogonal convolutions of size N×N𝑁𝑁N\times N obtained using a mapping that involves Fourier transforms, which leads to higher computational complexity even at inference. The change of support can also explain the slight difference in A​c​c.c​l​e​a​nformulae-sequence𝐴𝑐𝑐𝑐𝑙𝑒𝑎𝑛Acc.clean between the C​a​y​l​e​y𝐶𝑎𝑦𝑙𝑒𝑦Cayley method and the strong regularization λ=10𝜆10\lambda=10 for Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} method.

Figure 6 presentsthe same experiments on the Imagenette data set  (Howard, 2020). The latter is a 10-class subset of Imagenet data set (Deng et al., 2009) with 160×160160160160\times 160 images. The architecture is also a VGG-like one but with 15 convolutional layers.We trained 101010 times during 400400400 epochs with a batch size of 646464, using the same loss and optimizer as for the Cifar10 experiments. The architecture and hyperparameters are described in Section H.2. The average performance of the unconstrained C​o​n​v​2​D𝐶𝑜𝑛𝑣2𝐷Conv2D networks is about 83%percent8383\%.

Interestingly, even if the image size is larger on the Imagenette data set, Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} regularization shows the same profile as for Cifar10, when λ𝜆\lambda decreases, ranging from strong orthogonality to high clean accuracy (equivalent to Conv2D configuration), with the best compromise for λ=10−3𝜆superscript103\lambda=10^{-3}. Notice that for λ𝜆\lambda decreasing from 101010 to 0.010.010.01 the loss of orthogonality permits to obtain a significantly better accuracy but does not significantly favor attacks. Altogether, this leads to an increase in the empirical robustness accuracy. The phenomenon is present but less visible on Cifar10.

Besides, because Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} does not depend on the size parameter N𝑁N of the input channels, the processing time for the Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} regularization is only 1.11.11.1 times slower than for the non-constrained convolution C​o​n​v​2​D𝐶𝑜𝑛𝑣2𝐷Conv2D. In comparison, the C​a​y​l​e​y𝐶𝑎𝑦𝑙𝑒𝑦Cayley method is 6.56.56.5 slower than C​o​n​v​2​D𝐶𝑜𝑛𝑣2𝐷Conv2D.

This paper provides a necessary and sufficient condition on the architecture for the existence of an orthogonal convolutional layer with circular padding.The conditions prove that orthogonal convolutional layers exist for most relevant architectures.We show that the situation is less favorable with ‘valid’ and ‘same’ zero-paddings.We also prove that the minimization of the surrogate Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} enables constructing orthogonal convolutional layers in a stable manner, that also scales well with the input size parameter N𝑁N.The experiments confirm that this is practically the case for most of the configurations, except when M=C​S2𝑀𝐶superscript𝑆2M=CS^{2} for which interrogations remain.

Altogether, the study guarantees that the regularization with Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} is an efficient, stable numerical strategy to learn orthogonal convolutional layers.It can safely be used even when the signal/image size is very large.The regularization parameter λ𝜆\lambda is chosen depending on the tradeoff we want between accuracy and orthogonality, for the benefit of both accuracy and robustness.

Let us mention three open questions related to this article. First, a better understanding of the landscape problem as well as solutions to this problem when M=C​S2𝑀𝐶superscript𝑆2M=CS^{2} could be useful. Also, as initiated in  Kim et al. (2021); Fei et al. (2022), the extension of Lipschitz and orthogonal constraints and regularization to the attention-based networks is a natural and relevant open question. Finally, a clean adaptation of the regularization with Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} for the ’valid’ and ’same’ boundary conditions is needed.As shown in Section 2.2, approximate orthogonality seems to be key with these boundary conditions.

In this section, we give the definitions for signals (1D case), of the objects defined in the introduction for images (2D case).

As in Section C, we give the full proof in the 1D case and a sketch of proof in the 2D case.

In the RO case, the proof is based on calculations in which we carefully detail the structure of the matrix 𝒦​𝒦T−I​dM​N𝒦superscript𝒦𝑇𝐼subscript𝑑𝑀𝑁\mathcal{K}\mathcal{K}^{T}-Id_{MN} and identify its constituent with those of Lo​r​t​h​(𝐊)subscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎ𝐊L_{orth}(\mathbf{K}). The main lemma describing the structure of 𝒦​𝒦T−I​dM​N𝒦superscript𝒦𝑇𝐼subscript𝑑𝑀𝑁\mathcal{K}\mathcal{K}^{T}-Id_{MN} is Lemma 16. It is deduced from Lemma 14 and Lemma 15 which focus on submatrices of 𝒦​𝒦T−I​dM​N𝒦superscript𝒦𝑇𝐼subscript𝑑𝑀𝑁\mathcal{K}\mathcal{K}^{T}-Id_{MN}.

The result in the CO case is obtained from the result in the RO case and a known relation between ‖𝒦​𝒦T−I​dM​N‖F2superscriptsubscriptnorm𝒦superscript𝒦𝑇𝐼subscript𝑑𝑀𝑁𝐹2\|\mathcal{K}\mathcal{K}^{T}-Id_{MN}\|_{F}^{2} and‖𝒦T​𝒦−I​dC​S​N‖F2superscriptsubscriptnormsuperscript𝒦𝑇𝒦𝐼subscript𝑑𝐶𝑆𝑁𝐹2\|\mathcal{K}^{T}\mathcal{K}-Id_{CSN}\|_{F}^{2}, see for instance Lemma 1 in Wang et al. (2020).

Before proving Theorem 7, we first present three intermediate lemmas.

We start by stating intermediate lemmas.First we introduce a slight abuse of notation, for a vector x∈ℝN2𝑥superscriptℝsuperscript𝑁2x\in\mathbb{R}^{N^{2}}, we denote by 𝒞​(x)=𝒞​(X)𝒞𝑥𝒞𝑋\mathcal{C}(x)=\mathcal{C}(X), where X∈ℝN×N𝑋superscriptℝ𝑁𝑁X\in\mathbb{R}^{N\times N} such that Vect⁡(X)=xVect𝑋𝑥\operatorname{Vect}(X)=x.The main steps of the proof in the 2D case follow those in the 1D case and are given below.

Then we proceed as in the 1D case.

As in Section C and Section E, we give the full proof in the 1D case and a sketch of proof in the 2D case.

The lower-bound is a consequence of Theorem 7.

The proof of the upper-bound is different in the RO case and the CO case. In the RO case, we first express the orthogonality residual using Lemma 16. Then we conclude with calculations based on matrix norm inequalities, properties of circulant matrices and the definition of Lo​r​t​h​(𝐊)subscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎ𝐊L_{orth}(\mathbf{K}).

The CO case is more difficult since, to use in place of Lemma 16, we first need to establish Lemma 20. We then proceed with calculations to express that ‖𝒦T​𝒦−I​dC​S​N‖2subscriptnormsuperscript𝒦𝑇𝒦𝐼subscript𝑑𝐶𝑆𝑁2\|\mathcal{K}^{T}\mathcal{K}-Id_{CSN}\|_{2} is upper-bounded by a quantity independent of N𝑁N, as long as N≥2​k−1𝑁2𝑘1N\geq 2k-1. Then, after calculations, a key argument is to apply Theorem 7 to the matrix 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} obtained for the signal size N′=2​k−1superscript𝑁′2𝑘1N^{\prime}=2k-1.

The lower-bound of Theorem 8 is an immediate consequence of (8) and Theorem 7. In the RO case, we proceed as in the 1D case.In the CO case, we first prove a lemma similar to Lemma 20, then we proceed as in the 1D case.

Below, we prove Proposition 3 for a general matrix A∈ℝa×b𝐴superscriptℝ𝑎𝑏A\in\mathbb{R}^{a\times b} with a≥b𝑎𝑏a\geq b.In order to obtain the statement for a convolutional layer 𝒦∈ℝM​N×C​S​N𝒦superscriptℝ𝑀𝑁𝐶𝑆𝑁\mathcal{K}\in\mathbb{R}^{MN\times CSN}:In the RO case (M≤C​S𝑀𝐶𝑆M\leq CS): we take A=𝒦T𝐴superscript𝒦𝑇A=\mathcal{K}^{T}, a=C​S​N𝑎𝐶𝑆𝑁a=CSN, b=M​N𝑏𝑀𝑁b=MN.In the CO case (M≥C​S𝑀𝐶𝑆M\geq CS): we take A=𝒦𝐴𝒦A=\mathcal{K}, a=M​N𝑎𝑀𝑁a=MN, b=C​S​N𝑏𝐶𝑆𝑁b=CSN.

In this section, we describe the details of the experiments conducted on Cifar10 an Imagenette data sets.

The network architecture used for Cifar10 data set is described in Table 3 (1.1 million parameters). Conv2D layer will depend on the configuration: classical C​o​n​v​2​D𝐶𝑜𝑛𝑣2𝐷Conv2D for unconstrained reference configuration, C​a​y​l​e​y​C​o​n​v𝐶𝑎𝑦𝑙𝑒𝑦𝐶𝑜𝑛𝑣CayleyConv for C​a​y​l​e​y𝐶𝑎𝑦𝑙𝑒𝑦Cayley configuration(we use Trockman and Kolter, 2021, implementation),and Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} regularization for Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} configuration (we use our implementation according to Definition 1). Weight initialization is done according to Glorot uniform.

Task loss is the classical cross-entropy (CE). As described in Béthune et al. (2022), 1-lipschitz property of orthogonal neural network may prevent learning with CE and require introducing a temperature, i.e. multiply the network predictions/logits by a factor τ𝜏\tau. Experiments for C​a​y​l​e​y𝐶𝑎𝑦𝑙𝑒𝑦Cayley and Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} are done with τ=20𝜏20\tau=20 (and τ=1𝜏1\tau=1 for classical C​o​n​v​2​D𝐶𝑜𝑛𝑣2𝐷Conv2D).

We use classical data augmentation: random translation (±10%)\pm 10\%), random rotation (±15plus-or-minus15\pm 15 degree), random horizontal flipping (0.5 probability), random contrast modification ([0.8,1.2]0.81.2[0.8,1.2]). For affine transformation zero-padding is used when required. The initial learning rate is set to 0.03, and linearly decreased down to 3×10−43superscript1043\times 10^{-4}.

The Er​o​bsubscript𝐸𝑟𝑜𝑏E_{rob} and El​i​psubscript𝐸𝑙𝑖𝑝E_{lip} metrics are computed using the code provided by Trockman and Kolter (2021).

The network architecture used for Imagenette data set is described in Table 4 (1.2 million parameters). Conv2D layer will depend on the configuration: classical C​o​n​v​2​D𝐶𝑜𝑛𝑣2𝐷Conv2D for unconstrained reference configuration, C​a​y​l​e​y​C​o​n​v𝐶𝑎𝑦𝑙𝑒𝑦𝐶𝑜𝑛𝑣CayleyConv for C​a​y​l​e​y𝐶𝑎𝑦𝑙𝑒𝑦Cayley configuration(we use Trockman and Kolter, 2021, implementation),and Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} regularization for Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} configuration (we use our implementation according to Definition 1). Weight initialization is done according to Glorot uniform.

Task loss is the classical cross-entropy (CE). As described in Béthune et al. (2022), 1-lipschitz property of orthogonal neural network may prevent learning with CE and require introducing a temperature, i.e. multiply the network predictions/logits by a factor τ𝜏\tau. Experiments for C​a​y​l​e​y𝐶𝑎𝑦𝑙𝑒𝑦Cayley and Lo​r​t​hsubscript𝐿𝑜𝑟𝑡ℎL_{orth} are done with τ=20𝜏20\tau=20 (and τ=1𝜏1\tau=1 for classical C​o​n​v​2​D𝐶𝑜𝑛𝑣2𝐷Conv2D).The initial learning rate is set to 5×10−45superscript1045\times 10^{-4}, and linearly decreased down to 5×10−65superscript1065\times 10^{-6}.

Input images are normalized per channel using the recommended mean and std ([0.485,0.456,0.406]0.4850.4560.406[0.485,0.456,0.406], [0.229,0.224,0.225]0.2290.2240.225[0.229,0.224,0.225]). The only data augmentation used is random horizontal flipping (0.5 probability).

In this appendix, we describe methods for computing singular values of a 2D layer transform matrix, with or without stride. The codes are provided in the DEEL.LIP141414https://github.com/deel-ai/deel-lip library.

For convolutional layers without stride, S=1𝑆1S=1, we use the algorithm described in Sedghi et al. (2018). We describe the algorithm for 2D convolutional layers in Algorithm 1. The algorithm provides the full list of singular values.

For convolutions with stride, S>1𝑆1S>1, there is no known practical algorithm to compute the list of singular values σ𝜎\sigma. In this configuration, we use the well-known power iteration algorithm associated witha spectral shift to compute the smallest and the largest singular value (σm​i​n,σm​a​xsubscript𝜎𝑚𝑖𝑛subscript𝜎𝑚𝑎𝑥\sigma_{min},\sigma_{max}) of 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K}. We give the principle of the algorithm in Algorithm 2. For clarity, we assume a function ’λ𝜆\lambda = power_iteration(M𝑀M,ui​n​i​tsubscript𝑢𝑖𝑛𝑖𝑡u_{init})’, that applies the power iteration algorithm to a symetric matrix M𝑀M starting from a random vector ui​n​i​tsubscript𝑢𝑖𝑛𝑖𝑡u_{init}, and returns its largest eigenvalue λ𝜆\lambda. In practice, of course, we cannot construct M𝑀M and the implementation must use the usual functions that apply 𝒦𝒦\mathcal{K} and 𝒦Tsuperscript𝒦𝑇\mathcal{K}^{T}. A detailed python implementation is provided in the DEEL.LIPlibrary.
